      ·  any relevant information that will explain or support your comments
For further information, please refer to the Hearing Order on the NEB website at www.neb-one.gc.ca.
                                                               Comments
                                                       Attach additional pages if necessary.
Transport Canada has reviewed the proposed project and identified that our department likely has regulatory
responsibility for the proposed Anchor Loop related to the following Federal legislation and associated regulations:
-Navigable Waters Protection Act
-National Energy Board Act
-Railway Safety Act and
-Canadian Environmental Assessment Act
Transport Canada will continue our review of the Application to the NEB and will be identifying any outstanding
information requests or data gaps to the proponent. Once our department has completed the review and received the
 necessary information, a Letter of Comment will be provided regarding the Application for the board's consideration.
It is also our understanding that a the NEB will have a preliminary environmental assessment screening report
available for review and comment on May 31, 2006. As a responsible authority for the project under the Canadian
Environmental Assessment Act, our department looks forward to reviewing and commenting on the screening
document to ensure that the screening meets our decision making needs.
Should you have any questions, or require clarification, please contact the our office.
Regards,
Kelly Hunnie
Environment Officer
Transport Canada

LETTER OF COMMENT
Submitted by J OAN KEHR
IN THE MATTER OF the National Energy Board Act and
the Regulations made hereunder,
AND IN THE MATTER OF an application Terasen Pipelines (Trans
Mountain) Inc; TMX ~—- Anchor Loop Application of 17 February 2006
AND IN THE MATTER OF National Energy Board Hearing Order OH~1~
2006 dated 19 April 2006 (File No. A—FP—PA-PA~TTM—2006 1 (3200—T009~2)

STATEMENT OF INTERST
I am a citizen of Canada and resident of Alberta.
LETTER OF COMMENT
I would like to bring to the Boards’ attention the ﬁndings of a yet unpublished thesis from
the University of Alberta. It is titled Effects Of Chronic Industrial Noise Disturbance 0n
Boreal F crest Songbirds. This thesis was written by Lucas David Habib as a partial '
fulﬁllment of the requirements for the degree Master of Science in Environmental
Biology and Ecology and has been successfully defended. The thesis will be published in
the fall of 2006. Enclosed you will ﬁnd a copy of the title page and abstract. At this time
full texts may be obtained from Stan Boutin at the University. Stan has agreed to forward
COpies to interested parties upon request. Contact him at sboutin@uaiberta.ca. Altemately
I suggest contacting Dr.Erin Bayne, MrHabib’s thesis adviser for further information.
This thesis has found that the breeding success of certain species of birds is negatively
impacted by chronic industrial noise. The wildlife of our region is already subj ect to
many new environmental stressors. (Climate change is just one of many that comes to
mind.) I suggest adding continuous loud noise to their home is unnecessary.
Other research has found Contihﬂous ioud-souhds are stressful to humans. Such sounds
trigger the body’s f1 ght or ﬂight reﬂex. As such, continuous exposure to loud noise is
harmful to many body processes including the Immune system, Are other animals so
affected? The answer is likely yes.

I suggest that Terasen’s Environmental Assessment Report’s conclusion that noise levels
Will only displace Wildlife until they are acclimatized is not accurate. I suggest many
species Will avoid those areas all together or suffer the consequences as some birds
obviously (10. Further, Who wants to own the pasture next to the Wolf Pump Station and
deal with the cost of stressed cattle?

, As the province eentinues to be blanketed by industrial development I suggest all
industry should be sensitive to the impact of noise on all creatures. Will the cumulative
impacts of noise pollution endanger the wildlife of entire ecosites and regions? I suggest
the Board ere on the side of caution. I suggest the Board ensure state 0f the art
soundprooﬁng is a design component of the two compressor stations that are proposed as

, part of the Anchor Loop project. I further suggest that all future proj ects be held to a
similarly high standard. '

University of Alberta
Effects of Chronic Industn'al Noise Disturbance on Boreal F crest Songbirds

by

Lucas David Habib

A thesis submitted to the Faculty of Graduate Studies and Research
in partial fulfillment of the requirements for the degree of

Master of Science

in
Environmental Biology and Ecology
Department of Biological Sciences .
Edmonton, Alberta
Fall 2006

Abstract

As energy development expands into Alberta’s boreal forest, sources of
chronic industrial noise also increase. I found that many passerine species are present
in lower densities in areas impacted by chronic industn'al noise; over one-third of
species analysed had lower abundances in these areas. Passerines as a whole had a
37% reduction in density near compressor stations. Male Ovenbirds (Seirus
aurocapz'lla) were 15% less likely to successﬁllly pair with a female in noise—affected
areas. T here was an there was an 18% increase in the number of ﬁrst-year breeders in
noisy areas, implying that competition with older birds may be forcing younger birds
out of optimal, quieter habitat. Finally, where birds may learn to alter their song’s
frequency in areas with substantial background noise these effects may be minimised;
Swainson’s Thrush (Catharus ustulatus) appears to have this ability although
Ovenbird and Red~eyed Vireo (Vireo olivaceus) do not.

LETTER OF COMMENT
Submitted by: JOAN KEHR
IN THE MATTER OF the National Energy Board Act and the

: v AND IN THE-MATTER OF - an applicatien: Teraseit: Pipelines (Trans 2 . - .. . .
.v ' _ ' Mountain) Inc. TMX ?Anchor Loop Applieation of 1?.Fehmary 2006 . .. I . .
I ' AND IN THE MATTER OF National. Energy Board Hearing Order OH-14— 9- -
2006 dated 19 Apri12006 (File No. A~FP~PA-PA—TTM~2006 1 (3200»T009-2)
STATEMENT OF INTEREST
I am a concerned member of the Alberta public. I feel I am one of many Who wish to see
improved environmental stewardship. Polls consistently ﬁnd protection of the
environment is one of Albertans top concerns.
AUTHOR BACKGROUND
Please be advised that I, J oan Kehr was present during meetings 3 and 4 of the Terasen
Pipelines public consultation process. As such, I was privy to the information presented
at those times and as Well as other information shared With me during conversations with
company ofﬁcials. I hold a B.Sc. in F orestry from the University of New Brunswick
LETTER OF COMMENT
My purpose in writing this letter is to advocate for greater safety features than those
proposed for this proj ect.
The requests and suggestions herein arise from the following proj ect risk factors:

1. The proposed 36” diameter line, when fully commissioned will handle 300,000
barrels per day (b/d) of liquid hydrocarbons. That in itself is a large volume but if the rest
of the Trans Mountain system is built to match it I am sure this number Will seem small.
That said 3 ml} WW lasting 19 minute at e: 9335173538.?3436OBOGsOOGb/d woulésee. .
2,083 bmeis (218,000 1.) of product spilt. This number would not include the volume of
ﬂow~down from either end of the rupture.

2. Further to the above What are the chances of an expedient cleanup in the event
of a spill? If the product ﬂows into one of the maj or rivers it parallels, it was noted by
Terasen, that during certain. periods of the year spill containment is. either limited or
impossible. This can happen during ﬂood conditions? spring runoff and Where ice

conditions make it too dangerous for Cleanup work to proceed. I don’t want to think about
what percent of the average year that entails. Perhaps the Board should.
3. In this climate of global unrest there is a very real, though small risk of
sabotage. This section of pipeline has many miles of isolated line making it an easy
target. The line itself could be considered a very attractive target given (I understand)
most of the product Will be destined for foreign markets. Remember not that long ago
Alberta had its’ own home-grown saboteur _ Wiebo Ludwig.
,. v ' 4. There is always arisk of line failure through design aha constructionﬂaws as ' . ' -
. V , well as 3rd party interference. IfNASAzcan-make costly miStakes so can pipeliners . r " - ' 3- ~ "
V 5. There is a risk of environmental damage from spills to the Wildlife and people
Who rely on the Athabasca and FraserRiver basins for their water source. Perhaps a study " '
should be commissioned into the effect of a large spill on the Fraser River salmon run.
6. Municipalities and citizens can be directly affected in the event of a large
waterborne spill. Will local governments have the personnel and resources to deal
effectively with the situation? Will the tap water be safe for drinking, washing or
bathing? What are the physical and emotional costs to the vulnerable members of our
society? What are the timelines for recovery? What damage will remain after all best
efforts? The bigger the spill, the bigger the problems.
7. No environmental bonds are required for this type of proj ect. One can only
hope that the pipeline owner at the time of any incident is solvent and in possession of a
good insurance policy or a large bank account. I am sure the Board realizes asset
divestiture near the end of an asset’s lifespan to small poorly capitalized businesses is
very common. No law I know of prevents this practice. The mining industry is full of
such examples and many expensive cleanup proj ects are now a government
responsibility.
Requests and suggestions re:
Pipeline Size
The proposed diameter of the line (3 6”) precludes the use of instant (explosive) valving. .
This type of valve could be instrumental in reducing the quantity of product released in
the event of a signiﬁcant line failure. The diameter of the line should be restricted to the
size of line for Which reliable instant shutoff valves are available. Those valves should be
used extensively throughout the system.
Valving
As a member of the consultation process I am. familiar With the valVih-g preposed for this
line. If the 36” diameter line is approved I suggest the proposed spacing and power
sources of the block valves needs to be reevaluated, I advocate a larger number of block.
valves be required With dual power sources for assured remote shutdown. Who wants to
wait for an employee to drive up to an hour or more to proceed with a manual closure? I
also advocate that the allowances for backﬂow volumes be reduced. This would
necessitate the positioning of more check valves on this line.

Spill Containment
There was no evidence presented at the consultation meetings that the Cleanup capacity
has been engineered to ensure that the volumes involved with a maj or rupture could be
effectively dealt with. I saw no analysis of how many vacuum trucks per hour would be
v needed for various spill sizes at a given control point. Is there room at the identiﬁed
' ' v _ .. control points tohandle thosevehides? Is there room for. the. necessary transfer. vessels. if - . .
' a V ' " required? How many pnmpsof what size wonldbe needed?I .gotthe distinotfeeling it . . ~ ..
' . was a “we’ll deal with it when the issue arises’ type of attitude. I’d ratherthe company ' -'
' ' dealt with it ahead of time. 5 * ~ ~ ? o.
On this same topic I would hepe to see some sort of analysis that assured the public all
the necessary spill mitigation materials were within x hours of any location. That those
materials were sufﬁcient to contain a maj or discharge until the next available source of
materials could be brought on site.
Then of course there is the issue of training. Anything I saw presented by the company
indicated their cleanup simulations involved one small pump and an equally small
transfer tank. Something is better than nothing but is this something inadequate given the
potential volumes involved? Is there enough training ...particularly that involving local
community services? No training has involved Hinton protective services in at least the
last 5 years. I suggest more and better preparedness training should be expected.
Control Station Shutdown Procedures
Product throughput in this line is controlled at the company’s Edmonton terminal. If
anomalies in the line pressures and or volumes are detected employees are required to
immediately work through a systems checklist prior to instituting, if necessary, a systems
shutdown. Terasen ofﬁcials told me that this procedure takes about 10 minutes to
execute. Given that is the case, a line pumping 300,000b/d has a lot of potential for
putting a lot of product on the ground or in the water. I suggest that any anomaly over set
parameters be cause for immediate systems shutdown. This demand, if sufﬁciently
restrictive, would I’m sure, drive many improvements to the control system. Safety
systems improvements are a worthwhile objective.
Pipeline Integrity
I suggest that the proposed frequency of indine integrity testing be questioned. ?esting
frequency Should be based on risk and With this larger line and increasing magnitude Of
weather events there is more risk. Geometric deformation testing is one example where
more frequent testing might pay big dividends.

Right of Way Monitoring
: I suggest adding satellite imaging to the array of leak and hazard detection tools required.
3 A computer program that uses satellite imagery could, in a very timely manner, ﬂag
unusual activity or changes along the right of way.

'  ' ; V In, this letter I have'vvmadenumemus suggestienswith the obj ectiveﬁ imereved - ' i . " r. V --
" environmental protection; These come ,fromsomeone with no engineering or energy . .
industry background. Like all ideas they need to he critiqued and alternate solutions .
considered. While doing so, please remember we only have one world and we should
treat it like the treasure it is.
In this letter I have noted the increased risk to the environment that comes with this
: project. I do not feel the proposed design and operational features are adequate given the
risks involved. I feel this company can and should do better. When society expects more
i and demands more it gets more. Signiﬁcant improvements to the exhaust emissions of
{ automobiles were developed when the State of California legislated emission limits for
e vehicles sold in that state. All North Americans have beneﬁted from the actions of those
brave legislators. In respect to- this proj ect I see the National Energy Board operating
from the similar position of power. The Board has the authority to demand more of this
company and industry for the people of Canada. Please do so.

LETTER OF COMMENT
Submitted by: JOAN KEHR
IN THE MATTER OF the National Energy Board Act and the
Regulations made thereunder,
AND IN THE MATTER OF an application Terasen Pipelines (Trans
Mountain) Inc. TMX --~ Anchor Loop Application of 17 F ebruary 2006
AND IN THE MATTER OF National Energy Board Hearing Order OH~1-
2006 dated 19 April 2006 (F ile N0. A~FP-PA~PA-TTM~2006 1 (3200-T009-2)
STATEMENT OF INTEREST
I am a citizen of Canada. I am concerned the economic impact analysis presented by
Terasen is much too narrow in scope and could result in an inappropriate ﬁnding by the
Board.
AUTHOR BACKGROUND
Please be advised that I was present during meetings 3 and 4 0f the Terasen Pipelines
public consultation process and have in my possession a copy of Terasen Pipelines (Trans
Mountain) Inc. Environmental Assessment for the TMX~ Anchor Loop Proj ect. I hold a
8.80. in F orestry from the University of New Brunswick.
LETTER OF COMMENT
I do not believe this project will have a net positive effect on the economy or the
environment of the Province of Alberta or the Country of Canada. I do not believe the
economic analysis submitted by Terasen is adequate under present circumstances. It does
not address many of the cumulative economic impacts of this proj ect.
As a preface I feel safe to propose the following as indisputable facts.
Completion of this project will enhance Canada and Alberta’s export potential for
liquid petroleum products.
Completion 0f this project Will encourage the further development of the Alberta
oil sands.
Completion of this proj ect will encourage an even faster rate of conventional oil
and gas development.
That Alberta in general and Fort McMurray in particular are presently
experiencing an economic boom with no end in sight.
That the world’s climate is being and Will be signiﬁcantly impacted by global
warming brought on by extensive greenhouse gas releases.

CLIMATE CHANGE EFFECTS
Climate change is considered a fact not a theory. Canadians should be part of the solution
to the global issues surrounding climate change. Policies and projects that encourage an
escalating rate of hydrocarbon exploitation are not in the public’s interest. Factored into
this industry’s rate of return to the country should be the cost of extreme weather events
that are accompanying a warmer world. It will be difﬁcult to factor in the cost of
droughts? hurricanes, ﬂooding caused by high precipitation events, coastal ﬂooding due
to ice cap melting and water shortages caused by reduced glacial melt but a national
regulatory body should be at least consider it. It would not be that difﬁcult to contact
northern communities and businesses to ﬁnd some real costs associated with increasing
average temperatures. Ice highways are no longer providing reliable transportation
corridors and more and more freight must be ﬂown in.
In my opinion and that of many others, the world would be better served by a shortage of
hydrocarbons and the higher prices that would follow. This in turn would encourage
conservation and the development of renewable fuel sources. For example higher
hydrocarbon prices would make ethanol fuel a more attractive alternative. This in turn
would help to provide the prairie farmer an alternate market for their grains. Price
subsidies and a world oversupply of grains have driven many farmers out of business and
more yet, to the brink of bankruptcy.
HEALTH EFFECTS
This proj ect will have the net effect of encouraging tar sands developments. This comes
at a time when it is unknown if pollution from that resource development is the cause of
signiﬁcant health concerns for the residents of F 01t Chipewyan. It has been widely
reported (in one case on CBC Radio 1 on the May 5th, 2006 edition of Sounds Like
Canada) that residents of this northern community, downstream from the tar sands are
suffering from a wide number and variety of health issues (especially cancers). The
illnesses are in signiﬁcantly disproportionate numbers to the rest of the provincial
pepulation. Government ofﬁcials are looking at the issues. Studies have been initiated.
The health care system will provide treatment. People will suffer. It all Costs.
A community has a problem. Prudent people and their governments would attempt to
limit exposure to likely causes of ill health, not increase that exposure until a correlation
is proved or disproved. Is j ob creation so culturally sacrosanct that we play Russian
Roulette with peOples lives?
A recently released study has linked sulfur gas exposure such as those found in ﬂare
stack gas emissions to decreased fertility in cattle. The next step that is proposed is to
study the effects in humans. Must we approve more development projects that drive this
industry before the results are in? Decreased cattle fertility costs farmers. Human
stillbirths cost families. Shouldn’t our society ere on the side of caution?

RISK TO FISHERY RESOURCES

What Will happen to the Fraser River salmon run if this river is subj ect to a large spill?
Has the company presented research or funded research into the effects of petroleum
contamination on this resource? Has it been shown what the dollar costs are if one
salmon runs is lost for even one year?

LOCAL ECONOMIC IMPACTS

Please consider the state of the Alberta economy. It is booming. I suggest that this proj ect
will only increase the economic activity of the province at a time Where that activity Will
be a net detriment to individuals and communities.

I personally have been unable to ﬁnd a stucco contractor to ﬁnish the siding work on our
house. Our insurance company threatened- to withdraw all home coverage if this work is
not completed Within the next year. Wish me luck please.

The community I live next door to had to scale back its library expansion proj ect due to
escalating costs. I know of one town staff position that has remained unﬁlled for over a
year after numerous successful applicants had been unable to ﬁnd suitable housing at an
affordable price. Other communities have been unable to proceed with their infrastructure
proj ects due to cost increases and contractor shortages. At the time of writing the City of
Edmonton is deciding What previously approved and budgeted proj ects to cancel in the
face of 20+% per annum cost increases.

This spring the City of F ort McMurray approved a development pennit for a work camp
within the city limits. This is not a positive thing for any community. The council felt
they had no alternatives. This same city council is presently trying to deal With other
major infrastructure deﬁcits as a result of existing industrial development. The council is
now considering taking an active roll to slow down oil sands development. Perhaps they
will have an approved position on this in the near future. A call to conﬁrm this initiative
might be in order.

McMurray is for all intentioned purposes a single industry city whose economy is based
on a non~renewable resource. We all know what happens when a community’s nearby
resource runs out. This proj eat, if it proceeds; will encourage ﬁirther growth in Fort
McMurray area making the inevitable bust even bigger. What sort of legacy is that? Who
will pay for the social costs associated with that problem? Our children I expect.
1 have listed but a few of the problems and issues surrounding a ‘red hot’ economy. Many
of these problems and issues have real dollar costs. Someone should crunch some .
numbers, 10115 of numbers.

GOVERNANCE IMPACT

Will the government of Alberta beneﬁt from the immediate inﬂow of the wealth this
proj ect Will generate? Have they a need for those dollars? Governments are notoriously
bad at managing oil wealth. Who can forget the bumper stickers “God grant us another
oil boom and we promise not to piss it away this time”. It neatly summarizes What many
peeple feel happened during Alberta’s last oil boom.

The arrival of vast oil wealth, it can be argued, leaves politicians With no incentive to
develop none oil resources. It makes it easier for them to i gnore constituents and creates
less critical assessment of government by those same constituents. While impossible to
quantify these issues in dollars, they all lead to real costs.

CUMULATIVE EFFECTS IN THE BOREAL FOREST

Please consider the cumulative impacts of further, immediate oil and gas exploration 0n
the boreal forest. Plants and animals, soils and water are all negatively impacted by the
increasing disturbances of that very important resource. Unlike fossil fuels this forest is,
if not irreparably damaged, a multifaceted renewable resource. Any development in this
region should be considered very seriously.

I grant that the oil and gas sector has successfully worked to reduce its’ footprint in the
last 30+ years. I am sure more progress will be made but this industries impact will
always be felt. The rate of exploration ml; make a difference on the cumulative effects in
this environment. With time pressures the oil and forestry sectors do not collaborate as
fully as possible on the development of common access corridors. With less pressure and
more time new technologies and procedures that will reduce impacts can be developed,
can be properly tested and can be implemented. With more time the environmental
effects of the present development can be scrutinized and if necessary, changes made
before the mom of any identiﬁed problem is overwhelming.

As of yet not a single acre of land mined for oil sands has been certiﬁed as reclaimed.
After 20+ years of trying this should be Viewed as a signiﬁcant issue. Consider the
escalation rate of coal bed methane extraction. The impact of this technology on the
aquifers 0f the provinces has yet to be determined. Yet the rate of development only
escalates. N0 erring on the side of caution here.

Perhaps these working in the Alberta oil and gas industry should be required to submitted
a boreal forest cumulative impact assessment report prior to the Board ruling on any more
applications that Will effect this region.
Anything that will slow the rate of exploitation of Alberta’s oil and gas resources will
increase the likelihood that the developments When they do proceed are done in an
environmentally sound manner.

SOCIAL ATTITUDES

At present this society appears to operate With the assumption that job creation is the
most important responsibility of government. This despite the fact anyone Who has been
sick knows if you don’t have your health a job opportunity is worthless. I suggest that
this be extended to include the concept that anyone Without a healthy place to live will
soon ﬁnd that a job is a. small, albeit temporary, consolation.

Further this society appears to have an attitude of entitlement With regards to many
things, in particular hydrocarbon resources. What about generations to come? Are they to
be left with only the cleanup costs? The resources that this proj ect will drain from our
country should be shared With future generations.

CONCLUSIONS

Times are changing and so must the criteria by which the National Energy Board assesses
the development proj ects brought before them. I suggest the cumulative economic impact
assessment criteria of the past are too narrow. The broader cumulative impacts of this
proj ect must be considered.

Approval of this proj ect at this time will have a net negative effect on our climate, our
economy and our society. J ust because some of the costs are difﬁcult to calculate it
doesn’t mean they aren’t very real. An attempt should be made to quantify these present
and future costs.

Please consider the economic benefits that are presented in Terasen’s analysis. How
would these numbers compare if that proj ect were delayed by one or two decades or
generations? Jobs in the future might be needed even more. Oil Will cost more I’m sure.
Royalty rates in all likelihood Will be proportionately greater. The only true cost of
proj ect delay to the citizens of Canada is the opportunity cost and surely that is not
signiﬁcant in the face of the costs of proceeding as reviewed in this letter. I suggest it is
Terasen’s responsibility to show a not beneﬁt to the citizens of Canada. I suggest they
have not done so.
Will the Board please hold that, at this time, this project is not in the public interest?

Subiect: TMX Anchor Loop Proiect - ‘
On June 3, 2005 in response to a Federal Coordination Request from the Canadian
Environmental Assessment Agency (The Agency), Transport Canada identified that our
department anticipated having responsibility under Section 5 of the Canadian
Environmental Assessment Act (CEAA) to assess the environmental effects of the
proposed project, based on the information provided at that time. -
In our rele as a likely Responsible Authority, Transport Canada reviewed the TMX
Anchor Loop Project En vironmenta/ Assessment Report (November 2006) a nd provided
comment via The Agency in a letter dated January 16, 2006. In this correspondence,
our department identified that the proponent should ensure that applications be
submitted to Transport Canada for all works in, on, over, under or through navigable
waterways including pipeline and equipment crossings, as well as water intakes and
release pipes; It was also identified that. upon receipt of na'vigability determinations from
Transport Canada’s - Marine Safety Branch, the preferred crossing options (both
pipeline and equipment crossing) on each navigable waterway should be provided. . ‘
Transport Canada has also reviewed Volumes I, H and IV of the TMXAnchorLoop '
Pm/ecz‘App/Ibat/on to the Nat/bna/ Energy Board (February 2006). Our review identified
that the above requested information has not been provided to date, nor have '
applications for the proposed work been submitted to Transport Canada in accordance
with the Navigable Waters Protection Act (NWPA). in the absence of Applications
under the NWPA, Transport Canada does not have a CEAA Section 5 trigger and is
unable to provide comments as a Responsible Authority to the Nationa! Energy Board
(NEB) on the P’retiminaw Envirenmente! Screening Report. .

1

Transport Canada is requesting that the proponent comptete and submit Applications in
accordancewith the requirements of the NWPA, at their earliest convenience, to allow -
for our continued participation in the environmental assessment process and to ensure
that only one environmental assessment (as prepared by the NEB at the completion of
- their hearing process) is required for the proposed project.
Should you have any questions or concerns related to this letter, please contact the
undersigned.

COMMENTS ON NOISE RESPONSE

Context: Terasen Pipelines (Trans Mountain) Inc. Consultation and Comments on the EA Report, Volume IV (February
2006) — Response to Questions on Environmental Assessment Report — Health Canada, 0.1? (page 164)

Health Canada is not completely satisfied with the proponent’s response to 0.17

c-d) Construction noise duration may be of a significant duration; in their answer to f) the proponent states
“construction activity is only there for a short time period” but does not provide a quantitative estimate of this “short
time period.”

Health Canada requires an estimate of this “short time period” and the noise from this activity so that there can be
an evaluation of the potential impacts due to construction noise at sensitive receptors. Without this information,
Health Canada will not be able to identify areas of concern, nor provide recommendations to mitigate any identified
impacts. If the time period is too short to use the ISO/CSA based criterion, then Health Canada can use the requested
information to assess impacts using the EPA criterion provided in the draft fact sheet for noise issues.

e) The proponent should identify the applicable local noise by-|aw(s) relevant to construction activity. Such by-laws
may be sufficiently protective; however, their specifications on noise restrictions need to be identified.

f) The equation D.1 in CAN/CSA-ISO 1996-1:05 is not restricted to traffic noise, as indicated by the proponent. The Ldn
is adjusted for any noise source using an applicable rating level, however when traffic is the noise source of concern,
there is no adjustment to the Ldn. An alternative to calculating percentage highly annoyed using CAN/CSA-ISO 1996-
1:05 would be to estimate community reaction using a normalized Ldn (see our response to c) and d) above, as
described in the EPA document "Information on Levels..." pp D16-D22 (1974). This document is available on the
internet:

http://www.nonoise.org/|ibrary/levels74/levels74.htm

Our criterion is that if the normalized Ldn of an intruding noise is above 62 dBA, the effect is recommended as
significant, requiring mitigation, because widespread complaints have been found in community noise reaction
studies (cf. Figure D-7). The proponent should provide:

- estimates for noise levels based on previous measurements at similar construction sites and/or predicted from
source sound levels;

- a description of the methods used to obtain the estimated construction noise level, including an indication of
commercial software used; if source sound power levels are used, list their estimated values and give an indication of
how they were obtained;

- representative baseline, construction and baseline + construction noise values at the sensitive receiver locations
or (optionally) as noise contour maps, including: daytime (hours from 07:00 to 23:00) time-average sound level, night
time (23:00 to 07:00) time-average sound level, 24 hour time-average sound level (Leq) values, the 1 hour Leq from
22:00 to 23:00 (for construction noise on|y)correction factors to the 4 types of Leq values following the prescription in
U.S. EPA 1974 so that the EPA method can be used to predict qualitative levels of complaints;

- information to justify the use of these correction factors as per the EPA prescription (non-acoustical factors will
need to be determined via preliminary community consultations);

- mitigation measures during construction and the above relevant predicted noise levels after mitigation. In
particular, consideration should be given to whether there is a need for ambient noise level sensitive backup alarms
or alarms that can be adjusted by the vehicle operator; some description of the type of impacted residences should be
provided with an estimate of attenuation from outdoor to indoor sound levels;

- a community consultation and noise monitoring plan.

COMMENTS ON COUNTRY FOODS RESPONSE

Context: Terasen Pipelines (Trans Mountain) Inc. Consultation and Comments on the EA Report, Volume IV (February
2006) — Response to Questions on Environmental Assessment Report — Health Canada, 0.1 (page 158)

Preamble: Potential contamination of country foods due to activities associated with the project have not been
evaluated.

HEALTH CANADA’S ADDITIONAL COMMENTS:

The proponent’s response to question #1 indicates that country foods gathered from the project area are consumed by
aboriginals and by occasional anglers. In addition, local country foods are consumed in areas outside of the project
area.

In this brief response, in regard to country foods, paint and hydrocarbons were identified as potential contaminants
during the construction phase. However, potential contaminants during the operation phase were not identified. The
possibilities of pathways of contaminants into foods have also not been eliminated in the proponent’s response.

In order to establish whether or not further risk assessment of contaminants in country foods would be required, two of
the criteria for further assessment that must be satisfied are: (1) the presence of potential contaminants in foods, and
(2) a pathway of exposure of these contaminants to humans. These criteria are documented in the screening IeveI risk
assessment discussion in Volume 3, Chapter 8 of Health Canada’s publication entitled Canadian Handbook on Health
Impact Assessment. If one of these criteria is not met for any given contaminant, there is no further need for risk
assessment of that contaminant in country foods.

Very little information has been presented on potential country food contaminants, other than the information on the
construction phase mentioned above. For instance, it is not clear if the potential of naturally occurring contaminants
have been considered. In this regard, as one example, waterway changes could result in mercury contamination of
fish (in the form of methylmercury). These fish could, also, be caught in areas near, but outside of the project area.
The proponent should clarify whether all potential sources of country food contamination have been considered in
their assessment and whether there are any exposure pathways of. these contaminants operating. AII phases of the
project must be considered.

Dear Mr. Mantha: -
RE: Hearing Order OH~1~2006 -- Terasen Pipelines / Kinder Morgan Canada
TMX Anchor Loog AQQli-catio-n
LETTER OF COMMENT
As you are aware, Environment Canada (EC) participated in the environmental assessment
review of the Anchor Loop Project as per the Canadian Environmental Assessment Act (CEAA).
The scope of specialist or expert information or knowledge provided by EC to the Responsible
Authorities (RAS) and the overall review team has been within the Department’s mandate as
defined through specific statutes and regulations assigned to the Minister of the Environment and
through our Federal Authority (FA) role and responsibilities under the Canadian Environmental
Assessment Act.
EC is providing this letter of comment with respect to the effectiveness to mitigate the adverse
environmental effects of the Project on wetlands and- how proposed mitigation measures,
including compensation, would be eXpected to meet the objectives of the Federal Policy on
Wetland Conservation. EC appreciates the opportunity to provide feedback and recommendations
on this matter‘
Federal Policy on Wetland Conservation
The objective of the Federal Government with respect to wetland conservation is to “promote the
conservation of Canada‘s wetlands to sustain their ecological and socioeconomic functions, now
and in the future”.1 In support of the above objective, the Federal Government, in cooperation
with the provinces and territories and the Canadian public, will strive to achieve the following
goals:
0 maintenance of the functions and values derived from wetlands throughout Canada 3
. no net toss of wetlandfunctions on all federal lands and waters ;
o enhancement and rehabilitation of wetlands in. areas where the continuing toss er CM
degradation of wetlands or their fun-ctiehs- have reached Ciriti-cai ieveis :
o recognition of wetland functions in resource planning, management and economic :
decision-making with. regard to all federal programs, policies and activities 3
. eeeerement e? wettands 0? significance to Canadians m
. recognition of sound, sustainable management practices in sectors such as forestry and 1,
agricuiture that make a positive contribution to wetlands conservation while also 53%ng
achieving wise use of wetland resources a
i The Federei Poii'cy en Wetiand Conservation. 1991. By the Government of Canada. Environment Canada.
‘ , , gee
Canada WW

. utilization of wetlands in a manner that enhances prospects for their sustained and

productive use by future generatiens
Two of the seven strategies presented in the Federal Policy on Wetland Conservation pertain
directty to federal lands. Those strategies, and associated action items relevant to federal land
managers, are reproduced here.
Strategy 2: “Develop exemplary practices in support of wetland conservation and sustainable
wetland use to be incorporated in the design and implementation of federal programs and in the
management of federal lands and waters.”

" 6 Encourage actions to enhance wetland functions on feo‘erai lands and waters
especially in those areas of Canada where the continuing loss or degradation of wetlands
has reached critical levels, or where wetlands are important ecologically or socio—
economically to a region.

- Commit all federal departments to the goal of no net loss of wetland functions on federal
lands and waters, in areas affected by the implementation of federal programs where the
continuing loss or degradation of wetlands has reached" critical levels, and where federal
activities affect wetlands designated as ecologically or socioseconomically important to a
region. Due to local circumstances where wetland losses have been severe, in some
areas no further loss of any remaining wetland area may be deemed essential.

Strategy 3: "The federal government will continue to manage the use of National Parks, National
Wildlife Areas, Migratory Bird Sanctuaries, National Capital Commission lands and other federal
areas established for ecosystem conservation purposes so as to sustain their wetland functions
and natural processes."

. Require the creation of management plans which adequately reflect the special role of
the wetland resource on federal lands secured for ecosystem conservation purposes, and
the periodic review and update of these plans. Management of such wetlands should only
support those activities that are compatible with sustaining wetland functions.

. Commit federal land managers to the goal of no net loss of wetland functions in all
federal areas secured for conservation purposes.

. Protect these wetlands from impacts resulting from land or water use and environmental
quality changes, both internal and external to the federal area boundaries, by applying
the Federal Environmental Assessment and Review Process, by enforcing compliance
with federal regulations, by working cooperatively with other levels of government, non-
government organizations and the private sector and, if required, by intervening in legal
or decision-making processes. It should be noted that the Federal Environmental
Assessment and Review Process has been superseded since the writing of the Policy by
the Canadian Environmental Assessment Act.

. Encourage recreational, scientific, and educational uses of wetlands as long as these
uses are not detrimental to wetland functions and do not conflict with the purposes of the
area.

Mitigation Measures

To achieve no net loss of wetlands, the federal government adopted the wetland mitigation
framework established by the North American Wetlands Conservation Council (1998 and 2000).2
Proponents bf prej’eots en lands supporting wettahds are expected to implement a hierarchical
progression of mitigation alternatives, as follows:

2 North American Wettebds Ceasewatiee Cemcit {Caeaéa}. $998. Wetiam‘ tvtitigatiei‘z and Compereatiee, Proceeciings of
a National Workshop. Compiled by Kenneth W. Cox and Allison Grose. Report No. 98-1, Environment Canada and
Canadian Wildlife Service, Ottawa, Ontario.

North American Wetlands Conservation Ceencil (Canada). 2000. Wettand Mitigation in. Canada, A Framework for
Application. Edited by Kemeth W. Cox. and Allison Grose. issues Paper No. 2000-1. North American Wetlands
Conservation Ceencil (Canada), Ottawa», Ontario.

1. avoid damage or destruction of wetlands,

2. minimize the unavoidable impacts, and

8. compensate for residual impacts that cannot be minimized.

Compensation has been defined as action taken at another location to offset the negative impacts
of an undertaking on the functions and/or area of a particular wetland.3 Compensation efforts, in
turn, should implement the following hierarchical progression:

1. restoration of degraded wetlands (i.e., return of a totally or highly~altered natural wetland
to its previous function),

2. enhancement of existing wettands (i.e.. the increase of one or mere values of at! or a
portion of an existing wetland, through human actions), and

3. creation of a wetland (i.e., conversion of a non-wetland area into a wetland through
human action).

Compensation ratios (wetland restored/created to wetland lost) are typically set on a case specific
basis, however, the guidelines suggest that the ratios may be higher than the baseline 1:1 in
recognition of: society’s inability to create fully functioning wetlands, with certainty, owing to the
inherent risk in wetland creation and restoration and current deficiencies in the supporting
wetland science.4

Recommendation

The Kinder Morgan Responses to Comments Received on the Environmental Assessment
Report notes that with the implementation of the site specific mitigation measures and restoration
techniques detailed in the Anchor Loop Environmental Protection Plan (Section 5.2) and
Restoration Plan (Detail 3.1) as well as ongoing monitoring as part of the post construction
monitoring program, the assessment concluded that wetland function can be restored and that
the no net loss goal can be achieved. However, the Environmental Protection Plan and
Restoration Plan does not provide adequate details on the specific mitigation measures,
restoration techniques, and compensation measures therefore, it’s very difficult to determine the
effectiveness to mitigate for the adverse environmental effects of the Project on wetlands and
how the proposed measures would be expected to meet the objectives of the Federal Policy on
Wetland Conservation.

EC recommends that Terasen Pipelines/Kinder Morgan Canada:

1. provide a detailed assessment of residual wetland loss,

2. provide detailed wetland compensation measures including restoration of existing
degraded wetlands, enhancement of existing. wetlands, and creation of
replacement wetlands,

3. discuss how the proposed measures would be expected to meet the Federal Policy
on Wetland Conservation, _

4. detail opportunities through the 'Net Benefits Working Group“ for compensation
and» wetland habitat enhancement measures, and

5. provide a. monitoring program to monitor the success of the wetland compensatiott
to ensure wetland, function can be restored and that the no net loss of wetland
function can be achieved.

Further, it shoutd be noted that EC recommends a ratio of 3:1" compensation.
3 North Ameecah Wetlands Conservation Councit (Canada). 1998. Wetland Mitigation and Compensation, Proceedings of
a Nationai Workshop. Compiled by Kenneth W. Cox and Altison Grose. Report No. 98—1. Environment Canada and
Canadian Wildlife Service, Ottawa, Ontario.

. 4’ North American Wetlands Conservetien Councit (Canada). 2000. Wetland Mitigation in Canada, A Framework for

         KINDER MORGAN’S ANCHOR LOOP APPLICATION

 Larry Pratt: There’s so much going on here. I’ve never found it necessary to go out of Alberta
to find interesting things to write about or talk about. I sure taught a lot of students, but none of
